                "msl_citation": "Kopacz, N. (2022). <i>Data supplement to \"A Study in Blue: Secondary Copper-rich Minerals and their Associated Bacterial Diversity in Icelandic Lava Tubes\"</i> (Version 1.0) [Data set]. Utrecht University. https://doi.org/10.24416/UU01-I32Z95",
                "msl_datacite_version": "1.0",
                "msl_description_abstract": "Lava tubes on Earth represent some of the most enticing Martian analogue environments when investigating the possibility of past or present life on Mars. Lava tubes provide stable, sheltered environments, isolated and protected from the radiation on the surface. The microbial mats in these caves further regulate the environment for life, allowing various microbial communities with different metabolisms to coexist. This adaptation is so successful, one could imagine such a microbe-mineral continuum might occur on other planets. The data from this package is from investigated lava caves in Iceland. The project aims to correlate biological and mineralogical data to describe the interactions between the microbes and their geological substrates, to identify microbe-specific speleothems as biosignatures, and to develop a sampling technique in these fragile environments. This is achieved with a combination of X-ray fluorescence spectroscopy (XRF), DNA sequencing, scanning electron microscopy coupled with energy-dispersive X-ray spectroscopy (SEM/EDS), and confocal Raman microscopy.\n\nThe data is provided in 6 subfolders for 6 experiments/samples. Detailed information about the files in these subfolders is given in the explanatory file Kopacz_et_al_2022_Data_description. Contact person is Nina Kopacz - Researcher - k.a.kopacz@uu.nl",
                "msl_description_abstract_annotated": "<span data-uris='[\"https://epos-msl.uu.nl/voc/materials/1.3/igneous_rock_-_extrusive-lava\"]'>Lava</span> tubes on Earth represent some of the most enticing Martian analogue environments when investigating the possibility of past or present life on Mars. <span data-uris='[\"https://epos-msl.uu.nl/voc/materials/1.3/igneous_rock_-_extrusive-lava\"]'>Lava</span> tubes provide stable, sheltered environments, isolated and protected from the radiation on the surface. The microbial mats in these caves further regulate the environment for life, allowing various microbial communities with different metabolisms to coexist. This adaptation is so successful, one could imagine such a microbe-mineral continuum might occur on other planets. The data from this package is from investigated <span data-uris='[\"https://epos-msl.uu.nl/voc/materials/1.3/igneous_rock_-_extrusive-lava\"]'>lava</span> caves in Iceland. The project aims to correlate biological and mineralogical data to describe the interactions between the microbes and their geological substrates, to identify microbe-specific speleothems as biosignatures, and to develop a sampling technique in these fragile environments. This is achieved with a combination of <span data-uris='[\"https://epos-msl.uu.nl/voc/geochemistry/1.3/equipment-x-ray_spectrometer-x-ray_fluorescence_spectrometer\"]'>X-ray fluorescence</span> spectroscopy (<span data-uris='[\"https://epos-msl.uu.nl/voc/geochemistry/1.3/equipment-x-ray_spectrometer-x-ray_fluorescence_spectrometer\"]'>XRF</span>), DNA sequencing, scanning <span data-uris='[\"https://epos-msl.uu.nl/voc/microscopy/1.3/apparatus-electron_microscopy\"]'>electron microscopy</span> coupled with energy-dispersive <span data-uris='[\"https://epos-msl.uu.nl/voc/geochemistry/1.3/equipment-x-ray_spectrometer\"]'>X-ray spectroscopy</span> (SEM/<span data-uris='[\"https://epos-msl.uu.nl/voc/geochemistry/1.3/equipment-x-ray_spectrometer-energy-dispersive_x-ray_spectrometer\"]'>EDS</span>), and confocal <span data-uris='[\"https://epos-msl.uu.nl/voc/microscopy/1.3/technique-chemical_and_mineralogical_analysis-raman_spectrometry_-_chemistry_and_mineralogy\", \"https://epos-msl.uu.nl/voc/microscopy/1.3/technique-crystal_structure_analysis-raman_spectrometry_-_crystal_lattice\"]'>Raman</span> microscopy.\n\nThe data is provided in 6 subfolders for 6 experiments/samples. Detailed information about the files in these subfolders is given in the explanatory file Kopacz_et_al_2022_Data_description. Contact person is Nina Kopacz - Researcher - k.a.kopacz@uu.nl",

                "msl_citation": "Liu, J. (2022). <i>Permeability of bituminous coal to CH4 and CO2 under fixed volume and stress boundary conditions: Effects of sorption</i> (Version 1.0) [Data set]. Utrecht University. https://doi.org/10.24416/UU01-XOJCFK",
                "msl_datacite_version": "Version 1.0",
                "msl_description_abstract": "We report permeability data of a cylindrical sample of high volatile bituminous coal (25mm in diameter) with a clearly visible cleat system, to CH4 and CO2, performed under (near) fixed volume versus fixed stress conditions. To isolate the effect of sorption on permeability evolution, helium (non-sorbing gas) was used as a control fluid. All flow-through tests reported here were conducted under conditions of single-phase flow at 40℃, employing Terzaghi effective stresses of 14-35MPa. This experimental study aimed to gain a better understanding of the effects of fully coupled stress-strain-sorption-diffusion behaviour on permeability evolution in coal reservoirs during CO2-Enhanced Coalbed Methane (ECBM) recovery. Our results obtained permeability evolution versus changes in effective stress under both fixed volume and fixed stress conditions, showing an exponential correlation. Importantly, permeability (κ) obtained at similar effective stresses showed κ_helium > κ_(CH_4 ) >> κ_(CO_2 ), and κ measured at fixed volume is higher than fixed stress. This all demonstrates that permeability to CH4 and CO2, under in-situ boundary conditions, is strongly influenced by the coupled effects of a) self-stress generated by swelling, b) the change in effective stress coefficient upon sorption, c) sorption-induced closure of transport paths independently of poro-elastic effect, and d) heterogeneous gas penetration and equilibration, dependent on diffusion. Our results also show that the Walsh permeability model offers a promising basis for relating permeability evolution to in-situ stress evolution, using appropriate parameter values corrected for the effects of stress-strain-sorption-diffusion. These data are supplementary to: \nLiu, J., Spiers, C.J., 2022. Permeability of bituminous coal to CH4 and CO2 under fixed volume and stress boundary conditions: Effects of sorption. Submitted to Frontiers in Earth Sciences. ",
                "msl_description_abstract_annotated": "We report <span data-uris='[\"https://epos-msl.uu.nl/voc/rockphysics/1.3/measured_property-permeability\"]'>permeability</span> data of a cylindrical sample of high volatile <span data-uris='[\"https://epos-msl.uu.nl/voc/materials/1.3/sedimentary_rock-coal-bituminous_coal\"]'>bituminous coal</span> (25mm in diameter) with a clearly visible cleat system, to CH4 and <span data-uris='[\"https://epos-msl.uu.nl/voc/geochemistry/1.3/measured_property-carbon_c-carbon_dioxide\"]'>CO2,</span> performed under (near) fixed volume versus fixed stress conditions. To isolate the effect of sorption on <span data-uris='[\"https://epos-msl.uu.nl/voc/rockphysics/1.3/measured_property-permeability\"]'>permeability</span> evolution, <span data-uris='[\"https://epos-msl.uu.nl/voc/geochemistry/1.3/measured_property-helium\"]'>helium</span> (non-sorbing gas) was used as a control fluid. All flow-through tests reported here were conducted under conditions of single-phase flow at 40℃, employing Terzaghi effective stresses of 14-35MPa. This experimental study aimed to gain a better understanding of the effects of fully coupled stress-<span data-uris='[\"https://epos-msl.uu.nl/voc/rockphysics/1.3/measured_property-strain\", \"https://epos-msl.uu.nl/voc/analoguemodelling/1.3/measured_property-strain\"]'>strain</span>-sorption-diffusion behaviour on <span data-uris='[\"https://epos-msl.uu.nl/voc/rockphysics/1.3/measured_property-permeability\"]'>permeability</span> evolution in <span data-uris='[\"https://epos-msl.uu.nl/voc/materials/1.3/sedimentary_rock-coal\"]'>coal</span> reservoirs during CO2-Enhanced Coalbed <span data-uris='[\"https://epos-msl.uu.nl/voc/geochemistry/1.3/measured_property-carbon_c-methane\"]'>Methane</span> (ECBM) recovery. Our results obtained <span data-uris='[\"https://epos-msl.uu.nl/voc/rockphysics/1.3/measured_property-permeability\"]'>permeability</span> evolution versus changes in effective stress under both fixed volume and fixed stress conditions, showing an exponential correlation. Importantly, <span data-uris='[\"https://epos-msl.uu.nl/voc/rockphysics/1.3/measured_property-permeability\"]'>permeability</span> (κ) obtained at similar effective stresses showed κ_helium > κ_(CH_4 ) >> κ_(CO_2 ), and κ measured at fixed volume is higher than fixed stress. This all demonstrates that <span data-uris='[\"https://epos-msl.uu.nl/voc/rockphysics/1.3/measured_property-permeability\"]'>permeability</span> to CH4 and <span data-uris='[\"https://epos-msl.uu.nl/voc/geochemistry/1.3/measured_property-carbon_c-carbon_dioxide\"]'>CO2,</span> under in-situ boundary conditions, is strongly influenced by the coupled effects of a) self-stress generated by swelling, b) the change in effective stress coefficient upon sorption, c) sorption-induced closure of transport paths independently of <span data-uris='[\"https://epos-msl.uu.nl/voc/rockphysics/1.3/inferred_deformation_behavior-deformation_behaviour-poroelastic_deformation\"]'>poro-elastic</span> effect, and d) heterogeneous gas penetration and equilibration, dependent on diffusion. Our results also show that the Walsh <span data-uris='[\"https://epos-msl.uu.nl/voc/rockphysics/1.3/measured_property-permeability\"]'>permeability</span> model offers a promising basis for relating <span data-uris='[\"https://epos-msl.uu.nl/voc/rockphysics/1.3/measured_property-permeability\"]'>permeability</span> evolution to in-situ stress evolution, using appropriate parameter values corrected for the effects of stress-<span data-uris='[\"https://epos-msl.uu.nl/voc/rockphysics/1.3/measured_property-strain\", \"https://epos-msl.uu.nl/voc/analoguemodelling/1.3/measured_property-strain\"]'>strain</span>-sorption-diffusion. These data are supplementary to: \nLiu, J., Spiers, C.J., 2022. <span data-uris='[\"https://epos-msl.uu.nl/voc/rockphysics/1.3/measured_property-permeability\"]'>Permeability</span> of <span data-uris='[\"https://epos-msl.uu.nl/voc/materials/1.3/sedimentary_rock-coal-bituminous_coal\"]'>bituminous coal</span> to CH4 and CO2 under fixed volume and stress boundary conditions: Effects of sorption. Submitted to Frontiers in Earth Sciences. ",

                "msl_description_abstract": "This data package underlies the results published in 'Catalytic Pyrolysis of Polyethylene with Microporous and Mesoporous Materials: Assessing Performance and Mechanistic Study' by Johan H. van de Minkelis. The experimental section in the supporting information gives an detailed explanation how all data is generated. The data is sorted by analysis method and in each subfolder a python script is placed that can be used to plot the data. In each method folder an excel file is included with related information on the datafiles and to which figure the data is connected to.",

                "msl_citation": "Vollmer, I. (2023). <i>Unravelling Potential Reaction Intermediates during Catalytic Pyrolysis of Polypropylene with Microscopy and Spectroscopy</i> (Version 1.0) [Data set]. Utrecht University. https://doi.org/10.24416/UU01-90SYAI",
                "msl_datacite_version": "1.0",
                "msl_description_abstract": "While plastics-to-plastics recycling via melting and re-extrusion is often the preferred option due to a relatively low CO2 footprint, this technique requires a highly sorted waste stream and plastic properties can often not be maintained. Obtaining aromatics, such as benzene, toluene, and xylene (BTX), via catalytic pyrolysis of polyolefins, such as polypropylene and polyethylene, offers another attractive recycling technology. In this process, a discarded crude oil refinery catalyst (ECAT) was previously shown to lower the unwanted formation of deactivating coke species compared to a fresh crude oil refinery catalyst (FCC-cat), while yielding 20 wt.% aromatics from polypropylene. In this work, we study the underlying reaction mechanism for this chemical recycling process over the fresh and used refinery catalyst as well as a model system, not containing any zeolite material, using a combination of microscopy and spectroscopy. More specifically, by using in-situ fluorescence microscopy, in-situ infrared spectroscopy, in-situ ultraviolet-visible spectroscopy as well as ex-situ solid-state nuclear magnetic resonance, we observe highly fluorescent methylated aromatic intermediates that differ for the three catalyst materials under study both in their fluorescence, IR, UV-Vis, and NMR features. This detailed micro-spectroscopic comparison informs which potential reaction intermediates lead to increased coke formation. Our results suggests that a next generation of catalyst materials for this process would profit from a higher accessibility and a milder acidity compared to an FCC-cat and shows the great potential of using ECAT to reduce coking and obtain a BTX stream, which could be become the chemical building blocks for the manufacturing of e.g., plastics and coatings. ",
                "msl_description_abstract_annotated": "While plastics-to-plastics recycling via melting and re-extrusion is often the preferred option due to a relatively low CO2 footprint, this technique requires a highly sorted waste stream and plastic properties can often not be maintained. Obtaining aromatics, such as benzene, toluene, and xylene (BTX), via catalytic pyrolysis of polyolefins, such as polypropylene and <span data-uris='[\"https://epos-msl.uu.nl/voc/materials/1.3/analogue_modelling_material-elastic_modelling_material-synthetic_elastic_material-polyethylene_ldpe_foam\"]'>polyethylene</span>, offers another attractive recycling technology. In this process, a discarded crude oil refinery catalyst (ECAT) was previously shown to lower the unwanted formation of deactivating coke species compared to a fresh crude oil refinery catalyst (FCC-cat), while yielding 20 wt.% aromatics from polypropylene. In this work, we study the underlying reaction mechanism for this chemical recycling process over the fresh and used refinery catalyst as well as a model system, not containing any <span data-uris='[\"https://epos-msl.uu.nl/voc/materials/1.3/minerals-silicate_minerals-tectosilicates-zeolite\"]'>zeolite</span> material, using a combination of microscopy and spectroscopy. More specifically, by using in-situ fluorescence microscopy, in-situ <span data-uris='[\"https://epos-msl.uu.nl/voc/microscopy/1.3/technique-chemical_and_mineralogical_analysis-infrared_spectroscopy\"]'>infrared spectroscopy</span>, in-situ ultraviolet-visible spectroscopy as well as ex-situ solid-state nuclear magnetic resonance, we observe highly fluorescent methylated aromatic intermediates that differ for the three catalyst materials under study both in their fluorescence, <span data-uris='[\"https://epos-msl.uu.nl/voc/geochemistry/1.3/measured_property-iridium_ir\"]'>IR,</span> UV-Vis, and NMR features. This detailed micro-spectroscopic comparison informs which potential reaction intermediates lead to increased coke formation. Our results suggests that a next generation of catalyst materials for this process would profit from a higher accessibility and a milder acidity compared to an FCC-cat and shows the great potential of using ECAT to reduce coking and obtain a BTX stream, which could be become the chemical building blocks for the manufacturing of e.g., plastics and coatings.                 "msl_description_abstract": "To determine the effect of nanoconfinement on the geochemistry of water, we incorporate dielectric permittivity data of nanoconfined water into thermodynamic equilibrium models. We explored water self-ionization, ion pairing, pH, and aqueous speciation under nanoconfinement within the Earth. The data is organised into seven folders, each labelled as Figure X, where X corresponds to the figure number in the primary publication. Thermodynamic equilibrium modelling was conducted using the Geochemist Workbench (GWB) software, available at https://www.gwb.com/, and the PyGCC package, available at https://pypi.org/project/pygcc/. Detailed information about the files and methods employed can be found in the accompanying readme file.",

                "msl_citation": "Shinohara, T. (2024). <i>Mechanical and microstructural data used in: “The effect of strain rate on inelastic strain development in porous sandstones deformed under reservoir conditions”</i> (Version 1.0) [Data set]. Utrecht University. https://doi.org/10.24416/UU01-PRMUTT",
                "msl_datacite_version": "Version 1.0",
                "msl_description_abstract": "Subsurface human activities, such as fluid extraction from oil or gas sandstone reservoirs, frequently leads to surface subsidence and even induced seismicity, as observed in the Groningen Gas Field, the Netherlands. Subsidence is caused by elastic and inelastic reservoir compaction at depth resulting from the increase in effective stress due to pore pressure reduction. The inelastic compaction is partly caused by rate- or time-dependent processes, meaning that compaction may continue even if production is stopped. To reliably evaluate the impact of post-abandonment behavior, mechanism-based rate-/time-dependent compaction laws are needed. \nWe performed constant strain rate (rates of 10-3-10-8  s-1) and strain rate stepping (rates varying between 10-4 and 10-9  s-1) experiments under conventional triaxial conditions, on highly porous, clay-bearing Bleurswiller (as an analogue of the Groningen reservoir sandstone) and almost clay-free Bentheimer sandstones. Our results showed a systematic rate effect at differential stresses exceeding 40-50% of the peak differential stress in Bleurswiller sandstone, while this rate effect was only observed at differential stresses exceeding 70% of the peak differential stress in Bentheimer sandstone. Additional experiments investigating the effect of confining pressure, temperature and pore fluid pH, and microstructural analyses on undeformed and deformed materials, suggest that the observed rate effects are likely controlled by rate-dependent intergranular frictional sliding at lower differential stress, with an increased role of stress corrosion cracking at higher differential stress.\nThe data provided in this dataset include mechanical and microstructural data obtained on our Bleurswiller and Bentheimer sandstone samples. The mechanical data were obtained from 24 conventional triaxial compression experiments at constant strain rate. The microstructural data include backscatter electron (BSE) images obtained for one undeformed sample and two deformed samples, and electron dispersive X-ray spectrometry (EDX) element maps on an undeformed sample. \n",
                "msl_description_abstract_annotated": "Subsurface human activities, such as fluid extraction from oil or gas <span data-uris='[\"https://epos-msl.uu.nl/voc/materials/1.3/sedimentary_rock-sandstone\"]'>sandstone</span> reservoirs, frequently leads to <span data-uris='[\"https://epos-msl.uu.nl/voc/subsurface/1.3/surface_subsidence\"]'>surface subsidence</span> and even <span data-uris='[\"https://epos-msl.uu.nl/voc/subsurface/1.3/induced_seismicity\"]'>induced seismicity</span>, as observed in the Groningen <span data-uris='[\"https://epos-msl.uu.nl/voc/subsurface/1.3/subsurface_energy_production-hydrocarbon_energy_production-gas_field\"]'>Gas Field</span>, the Netherlands. <span data-uris='[\"https://epos-msl.uu.nl/voc/subsurface/1.3/surface_subsidence\"]'>Subsidence</span> is caused by elastic and <span data-uris='[\"https://epos-msl.uu.nl/voc/rockphysics/1.3/inferred_deformation_behavior-deformation_behaviour-inelastic_deformation\"]'>inelastic</span> reservoir <span data-uris='[\"https://epos-msl.uu.nl/voc/rockphysics/1.3/measured_property-strain\", \"https://epos-msl.uu.nl/voc/analoguemodelling/1.3/measured_property-strain\"]'>compaction</span> at depth resulting from the increase in effective stress due to <span data-uris='[\"https://epos-msl.uu.nl/voc/rockphysics/1.3/measured_property-pore_fluid_pressure\"]'>pore pressure</span> reduction. The <span data-uris='[\"https://epos-msl.uu.nl/voc/rockphysics/1.3/inferred_deformation_behavior-deformation_behaviour-inelastic_deformation\"]'>inelastic</span> <span data-uris='[\"https://epos-msl.uu.nl/voc/rockphysics/1.3/measured_property-strain\", \"https://epos-msl.uu.nl/voc/analoguemodelling/1.3/measured_property-strain\"]'>compaction</span> is partly caused by rate- or time-dependent processes, meaning that <span data-uris='[\"https://epos-msl.uu.nl/voc/rockphysics/1.3/measured_property-strain\", \"https://epos-msl.uu.nl/voc/analoguemodelling/1.3/measured_property-strain\"]'>compaction</span> may continue even if production is stopped. To reliably evaluate the impact of post-abandonment behavior, mechanism-based rate-/time-dependent <span data-uris='[\"https://epos-msl.uu.nl/voc/rockphysics/1.3/measured_property-strain\", \"https://epos-msl.uu.nl/voc/analoguemodelling/1.3/measured_property-strain\"]'>compaction</span> laws are needed. \nWe performed constant <span data-uris='[\"https://epos-msl.uu.nl/voc/rockphysics/1.3/measured_property-strain\", \"https://epos-msl.uu.nl/voc/analoguemodelling/1.3/measured_property-strain\"]'>strain</span> rate (rates of 10-3-10-8  s-1) and <span data-uris='[\"https://epos-msl.uu.nl/voc/rockphysics/1.3/measured_property-strain\", \"https://epos-msl.uu.nl/voc/analoguemodelling/1.3/measured_property-strain\"]'>strain</span> rate stepping (rates varying between 10-4 and 10-9  s-1) experiments under conventional triaxial conditions, on highly porous, <span data-uris='[\"https://epos-msl.uu.nl/voc/materials/1.3/minerals-silicate_minerals-phyllosilicates-clay\", \"https://epos-msl.uu.nl/voc/materials/1.3/unconsolidated_sediment-clastic_sediment-clay\"]'>clay</span>-bearing Bleurswiller (as an analogue of the Groningen reservoir <span data-uris='[\"https://epos-msl.uu.nl/voc/materials/1.3/sedimentary_rock-sandstone\"]'>sandstone</span>) and almost <span data-uris='[\"https://epos-msl.uu.nl/voc/materials/1.3/minerals-silicate_minerals-phyllosilicates-clay\", \"https://epos-msl.uu.nl/voc/materials/1.3/unconsolidated_sediment-clastic_sediment-clay\"]'>clay</span>-free Bentheimer <span data-uris='[\"https://epos-msl.uu.nl/voc/materials/1.3/sedimentary_rock-sandstone\"]'>sandstones</span>. Our results showed a systematic rate effect at differential stresses exceeding 40-50% of the peak differential stress in <span data-uris='[\"https://epos-msl.uu.nl/voc/materials/1.3/sedimentary_rock-sandstone-wacke-bleurswiller_sandstone\"]'>Bleurswiller sandstone</span>, while this rate effect was only observed at differential stresses exceeding 70% of the peak differential stress in Bentheimer <span data-uris='[\"https://epos-msl.uu.nl/voc/materials/1.3/sedimentary_rock-sandstone\"]'>sandstone</span>. The mechanical data were obtained from 24 <span data-uris='[\"https://epos-msl.uu.nl/voc/rockphysics/1.3/apparatus-deformation_testing-compression_testing-triaxial_compression_apparatus-conventional_triaxial_apparatus\"]'>conventional triaxial compression</span> experiments at constant <span data-uris='[\"https://epos-msl.uu.nl/voc/rockphysics/1.3/measured_property-strain\", \"https://epos-msl.uu.nl/voc/analoguemodelling/1.3/measured_property-strain\"]'>strain</span> rate.
